      "diagnostic_reasoning": "The erratic jumping of the crankshaft timing mark, combined with power loss, points to a fundamental issue with the engine's mechanical timing. While a worn distributor gear (as initially suspected by your mechanic) can cause erratic ignition timing, the crankshaft timing mark itself jumping suggests a problem further upstream. The primary suspects are a slipping torsional damper or a stretched timing chain/worn sprockets. A slipping torsional damper would cause the timing mark on the pulley to be inaccurate relative to the crankshaft's true position. A stretched timing chain or worn sprockets would introduce excessive play between the crankshaft and camshaft, leading to erratic valve and ignition timing, which would be reflected as a jumping timing mark. The FSM lists 'Crankshaft timing sprocket damaged' and 'Timing chain damaged' as possible causes for noise in the crankshaft area timed to engine speed, and 'Timing chain damaged' and 'Distributor gear damaged' for camshaft area noise timed to 1/2 engine speed.       "mechanic_instructions": "The erratic crankshaft timing mark is a critical symptom. Before tearing into the timing chain, we need to rule out a simpler external cause. This would make the timing appear erratic even if the internal engine timing is stable. Inspect the torsional damper for any signs of rubber deterioration, cracking, or slippage between the inner hub and outer ring. Incorrect positioning, such as engaging reverse when forward is selected, will cause the transmission to slip and eventually fail, leading to grinding noises due to improper gear engagement. This is a critical adjustment for both Hurth and Velvet Drive transmissions. The next step is to verify the shift cable installation and adjustment according to the manual.",
      "mechanic_instructions": "Your observation confirms a critical issue with the shift cable setup. When the remote control is in FORWARD, the transmission shift lever MUST be positioned over the 'F' (Forward) letter on the transmission case. If it's moving to 'R' (Reverse), the shift cable is either incorrectly installed or severely out of adjustment. This will cause the transmission to attempt to engage the wrong gear, leading to the grinding noise you hear. We need to correct this immediately to prevent transmission damage.\n\nFirst, identify your transmission type (Hurth or Velvet Drive). Then, follow the shift cable installation and adjustment procedure in the manual. For Hurth transmissions, refer to 'Shift Cable Installation and Adjustment' on Page 8C-5 (Source 1). For Velvet Drive transmissions, refer to 'Shift Cable Adjustment' on Page 8A-6 (Source 49), which directs you to SECTION 2C - 'MIE Models - Velvet Drive Transmissions' for the full procedure, starting on Page 2B-13 (Source 28).\n\nPay close attention to:\n1.  The correct anchor stud hole for the shift cable bracket (front hole for Velvet Drive, or '630' hole for Hurth 630 transmissions). (Source 4, Page 25; Source 26, Page 12)\n2.  Centering the shift cable play (backlash) before adjusting the cable barrel. (Source 4, Page 25; Source 20, Page 13)\n3.  Ensuring the remote control provides at least 2-3/4 in. (70 mm) of total shift cable travel. Insufficient travel will cause the transmission to slip and fail. (Source 21, Page 23; Source 30, Page 41)\n4.       "diagnostic_reasoning": "The user has confirmed a bent or damaged propeller shaft. This is a direct cause of vibration, poor performance, and increased fuel consumption. The FSM provides procedures for checking shaft straightness and emphasizes the importance of proper engine and shaft alignment to prevent damage to the transmission output shaft oil seal and bearings. A bent shaft must be replaced, and the propeller and couplings should be inspected for secondary damage.",
      "mechanic_instructions": "A bent or damaged propeller shaft will cause significant vibration, poor performance, and can lead to further damage to the transmission and other components. This shaft must be replaced. Follow these steps:\n\n1.  **Remove the Propeller Shaft:** Refer to your boat manufacturer's service manual for the specific removal procedure for the propeller shaft, strut, and shaft log.\n2.  **Inspect the Propeller Shaft (if not already removed):** With the shaft removed from the boat, place it on V-blocks and use a dial indicator to check for straightness at three or four places. If the dial indicator deflects over 0.004 in. (0.1 mm), the shaft must be replaced. (Source: 2000_mercury_mercruiser_marine/.splits_mercruiser_305_cid_5l_manual/mercruiser_305_cid_5l_manual_part18_p851-900.pdf, page 8D-17).\n3.  **Inspect the Propeller Shaft Coupling:** Check that the bore of the coupling is 90 degrees from the coupling flange. If the needle moves when checking with a dial indicator, replace the coupling. (Source: 2000_mercury_mercruiser_marine/.splits_mercruiser_305_cid_5l_manual/mercruiser_305_cid_5l_manual_part18_p851-900.pdf, page 8D-17).\n4.  **Inspect the Propeller:** Even if the propeller was previously identified as damaged, re-inspect it for any additional damage or issues with seating on the new shaft. Check blade diameter and pitch. (Source: 2000_mercury_mercruiser_marine/.splits_mercruiser_305_cid_5l_manual/mercruiser_305_cid_5l_manual_part18_p851-900.pdf, page 8D-16).\n5.  **Install New Propeller Shaft and Components:** Install the new propeller shaft, ensuring proper fit of the coupling to the shaft. Torque the propeller shaft coupling and transmission coupling (output flange) to 50 lb-ft (68 Nm). If equipped with setscrews, tighten securely and safety wire them. (Source: 2000_mercury_mercruiser_marine/.splits_mercruiser_305_cid_5l_manual/mercruiser_305_cid_5l_manual_part18_p851-900.pdf, page 8D-15).\n6.  **Perform Engine Final Alignment:** This is critical. The engine must be aligned so that the transmission output flange and propeller shaft coupler centerlines are aligned, and coupling faces are parallel within 0.003 in. (0.07 mm). This alignment must be rechecked with the boat in the water, fuel tanks filled, and a normal load on board. Refer to 'MIE MODELS' on page 2B-7 and 2B-8 for the complete engine final alignment procedure. The Knock Control Module (KCM) plays a critical role in ignition timing by signaling the ECM to retard timing when spark knock is detected. The KCM sends an 8-10 volt signal to the Ignition Control Module (or ECM) when no spark knock is present. If spark knock is detected, this signal is removed, prompting the ECM to retard ignition timing. A 'false constant spark retard' can occur if the KCM's 12-volt power supply is missing, preventing it from sending the 8-10 volt signal. Pay attention to the crankcase ventilation hoses connected to it, cleaning and inspecting them for cracks or deterioration as well. The most direct way to test this is to check the water temperature sender's accuracy. The FSM provides a procedure for testing the water temperature sender by comparing its resistance readings to known temperature values (Source 36, Page 22; Source 48, Page 21; Source 59, Page 21).       "mechanic_instructions": "You've confirmed the gauge reading is inaccurate. The next step is to test the water temperature sender itself.
